1579128955|5.0|If you're a mathematician by trade, education or inclination - or if you just enjoy science - then the periodic table is a thing of beauty.  It puts the universe in order in a wonderful fashion, giving it a logical basis that didn't exist prior to the table's creation.  It even successfully predicted elements that hadn't been discovered.  Theodore Grey's The Elements celebrates both the periodic table and the individual elements that make it up.<br /><br />After a few introductory pages that explain how the periodic table is constructed, the book dedicates two to four pages on each element, providing a few paragraphs about the significance of the element along with some nice pictures of the element either in pure form or in a compound or in some object.  Of course, with later elements (the very short-lived elements), there is very little to show or discuss.<br /><br />For each element, Gray provides some vital statistics (like atomic weight or density) along with graphical representations of the electron distribution, atomic emission spectrum and temperature ranges where the element is solid, liquid or gas.  Fanning out the pages will show that these ranges fluctuate based on atomic number and are not merely random.<br /><br />If you are into science, this is a nice book, but it may not have all the details you'd want.  It is more of an introduction to the foundation of chemistry, something to teach newcomers with nice illustrations and often humorous text.  Despite all the versions of periodic tables he shows in the book, the one thing I found Gray omitted was a complete table showing all the elements by symbol.  Nonetheless, this is a fun and informative book that is good for all ages to learn more the stuff of which we are made.

0307454541|2.0|Motivated by the opinions of some respectable reviewers and my current interest in international authors, I was predisposed to like the first of Larsson's trilogy. Maybe my high expectations also predisposed me to disappointment, but maybe not. My reading experience ranged from trying really hard to maintain attachment to feeling intellectually insulted. Larsson builds his mystery around the dark mind of sexually violent predators (SVP), their male and female victims and the dysfunctional family. Trying to connect with his protagonist was like chewing on styrofoam. Lisbeth, who I loved, is the intriguing figure and should have been the voice of the story. I disagree with reviewers who thought that the violent rape by her legal advocate was gratuitous. Larsson attempts to paint Lisbeth as textbook Asperger's with massive intelligence but no connection to human emotion other than her intense anger. Her lack of emotional response after the rape, other than to revenge it, is an effectual device that reveals her psychological core. Well done so far until she joins Blomkvist. Then Mikeal's mega-chic-magnetism supposedly provides the antidote for Lisbeth's syndrome. She becomes fascinated and aroused by him while I became like yeah, right. It's as believable as me (56 yo but still a Blomkvist stud...in my own mind) hooking up with disturbed teen emo-chicks. Not fantasy, absurdity. Throughout, with all due respect to those who love these books and consider them so cerebral, I found Larsson's writing amateurish and not at all intellectually stimulating. To be fair there are nuggets of insight and creativity, but these are nested within loads of trite devices, redundant descriptions, bland characters and simplistic, choppy prose delivered in sophomoric style. When an author chooses a subject as serious as the psychology of SVP and a victimized woman with Asperger's he owes the reader a serious treatment. Read Joyce's Ulysses instead.

While I love many of Stephen King's work, this tale will forever be one of my favorites. He is and always will be on my auto-buy list. B001S31MTQ|2.0|Here is Huxley's famous and influential work on the use of mescaline.  It consists of the record of a mescaline trip, where he was attended by a friend and his wife, and musings on the spiritual and historic uses of the drug.<br /><br />For the reader today, the book will seem quite tame and matter-of-fact, but for someone like Huxley to speak out on this drug, in his time, was quite controversial.  I did enjoy the essay though I think it suffers from a fatal flaw:<br /><br />To the already initiated user of the drug, the book is unnecessary, as the effects he describes and the conclusions he reaches are nearly universal among users I have known.  On the other hand, to the uninitiated reader, the book will read like so much nonsense.<br /><br />It is not terribly different than to try to explain a personally transcendent experience of any kind to those who have not experienced one of their own.  It was Huxley's goal to penetrate academia with this book and motivate research into what he considered a very important drug for mankind.<br /><br />The second half of the book (more or less) is a second essay, \\"Heaven and Hell\\", which discusses, basically, the transcendent nature of art (mostly) and the draw toward certain mediums, themes, and materials in history.  It is a study in philosophical idealism.<br /><br />I found that it wandered, did not well support its case, and was over-long.

The author is winking at us. Heck, he has a cleverness tic.<br />And we readers, an eager bunch, catch the wink like a kiss. We nod at all the allusions to Nietzsche and his Eternal Recurrence, to Spinoza and his God or Nature, to the back and forth to where we were, to the darn memories melting into time like tears in the rain. We note carefully which character has the birth mark and trace the evolution of that soul.<br /><br />So grouses cynical me. Appreciative me notes the nicely done changes of voice from one chapter to the next. I also love the occasional beautifully crafted moment which can be breath-taking.<br /><br />\\"As many truths as men. Occasionally, I glimpse a truer Truth, hiding in imperfect simulacrums of itself, but as I approach, it bestirs itself & move deeper into the thorny swamp of dissent\\" p.17.<br /><br />Very nice but nothing that many others could not have done as well. I can page through my commonplace books and find quotes from Churchill, Ross McDonald and Montaigne that are as true. I admit that is a fine company. But whatever philosophy is expressed in his writing is as inchoate as his beloved clouds.<br /><br />Ours is a time begging for transcendence. How many of us have picked up a book hoping that it can make sense of our lives?<br />Still I can understand why this book was made into a movie. The visual artistry will add to the sense of mythic poetry. However, computer imagery is not meaning. B009UVWBMA|5.0|* The following review appears on the main \\"Red\\" page as well but is a review of this version which includes the extra tracks available here on what is the Target Exclusive version *<br /><br />There are going to be hundreds of reviews of this album out there (and yes, I'm old, I still call them albums) and I know I'm not in Taylor's main demographic audience. First off, I'm a guy. Second off I'm 42 years old and normally listen to things like Irish punk, The Beatles, Springsteen or some hard core country like Chris Knight. I love big band music whether it's Frank Sinatra or Big Bad Voodoo Daddy. With all of that said, I adore Taylor and this new album \\"Red.\\"<br /><br />Taylor's songwriting is still in fine form here. The thing that always amazes me about her writing is that she's able to tell stories in her songs, she keeps it simple and more often than not finds one heck of a hook in the chorus. There is passion, there's energy and there is joy. Sure, there are so sad songs, but it seems to my that Taylor takes so much joy in making music that it's hard as a listener not to get caught up in that with her.<br /><br />After listening to the 16 tracks and the 6 bonus tracks available on some packages I have to say that Taylor's bonus tracks alone are better than what most artists are putting out as albums these days. The \\"demo\\" recordings of \\"Treacherous,\\" \\"Red\\" and \\"State of Grace\\" are excellent and polished works that are just a bit more stripped down than the versions that appear on the album. It's not really fair to call them demos, as when I hear that word I'm expecting below-par sound and performance. I think it's the mark of a good song if it can be done solo-acoustic (or at least very stripped down) and these three songs certainly hold up well.<br /><br />For me the whole album is very solid, there are no songs on this disc that I'd skip when listening to it. There is a nice mix of slower ballads and hard hitting rock numbers. Make no mistake about it, Taylor Swift is a rock and roll star. She's setting the bar for what pop music is and doing a fine job of it. It's funny, while listening to \\"Red\\" and the big anthmatic numbers I couldn't help but think this is the modern day version of Phil Spector's wall of sound. Sure, there are a lot of synthesizers here, but Taylor has an uncanny ability to create a big sound that just envelops you, especially if you like to play your music as loud as I do.<br /><br />I think it's the fact that Taylor is such a good songwriter and maybe more importantly storyteller that makes her so accessible across so many different lines. It doesn't matter how old you are or what gender you are, Taylor's songs resonate. Even a song like \\"22\\" which is an anthem for the young \\"let's go out and party\\" crowd it still hits home with me at 42, because believe it or not kids I was 22 at one point. And, I'm guessing the 10 year olds that hear the song will relate to it because they know it's exactly what they're going to want to do when they're 22.<br /><br />Red is a little bit country, a lot of pop and a heavy dose of rock and roll. Like some big artists before her, Taylor Swift is not a follower, but instead she is creating the standard for what is popular music. And maybe this is the old man in me coming out, but where most of popular music is fairly brainless and devoid of substance that's the opposite of what Taylor Swift brings to the table. She brings some great story songs, most of which (if not all) I'm pretty sure are very auto-biographical. Taylor is simply telling us her story, she's just making it rhyme and adding some killer hooks and melodies for good measure.Swift is a rock and roll star. She's setting the bar for what pop music is and doing a fine job of it. Album three was not to be as the record company decided to drop him, just before Christmas.<br /><br />Meanwhile in a record store over in South Africa we have a story of a legend who has died in mysterious circumstance and a man who made it his mission to find out the truth about his unsung American hero. Rodriguez and his anti establishment attitude, easily accessible lyrics and effortless vocal style had made him an underground hit in apartheid struck South Africa. He was seen as a man who spoke for those who saw segregation as an unnecessary evil. His records had not only been banned but defaced by the authorities so that the offending material could never be played on air. Instead it took off underground, even with bootleg tapes etc. Then they heard he had shot himself, then he had burnt himself and the mystery of the man behind the shades deepened.<br /><br />This documentary tells of the amazing story to find a man that had inspired a nation but who never knew about it. It features a lot of his music and some live performances, and it is just inspirational. I am going to order his records after hearing some of them in the film. This is one of those films that you will want to tell your friends about, it has that rare quality of being a true story that you feel just has to be made up, but is all the more brilliant because it isn't. If you love music, good films or just a great heart enriching story then you will adore this great piece of history.

B002GHHHG0|5.0|Legendary horror icons George A. Romero and Stephen King team up to create an anthology classic in 1982's tribute to the horror comics of the 50's, \\"Creepshow\\".  Bookended by a dark story of a boy punished by an abusive father for reading a Creepshow comic book, this fan-favorite film goes on to tell five stories of terror to chill your bones! In \\"Father's Day\\", an awful old man takes revenge for his murder from beyond the grave. In \\"The Lonesome Death of Jordy Verrill\\", a country simpleton falls prey to an ever growing fungus from outer space. \\"Something to Tide You Over\\" is a revenge upon revenge tale of adultery and its consequences for everyone involved. \\"The Crate\\" basically takes the Looney Tunes' Tasmanian Devil cartoons as fodder for what is probably the best segment in the film, a story of a man embarrassed and brow-beaten by an awful woman, and what he does about it when he discovers a seemingly perfect solution. And, finally, \\"They're Creeping Up On You,\\" a tale of a Scrooge-like man with a germ phobia whose supposedly bug proof apartment becomes overrun with the critters!<br /><br />\\"Creepshow\\" is a really fun horror anthology (my favorite type of horror film) from beginning to end, though not all the tales are equal. The wraparound is fascinatingly creepy. Father's Day is, in my opinion, the weakest of the bunch. I just don't find it that interesting. Jordy Verrill is close in the running for best story. It's downright disturbing, silly as it is (and King plays the lead!). Something to Tide You Over is a great one in the Amicus anthology tradition, and with a great cast, too! The Crate is most likely the favorite for most folks, though I'm just guessing, because the idea is just fantastic and it is well played out. They're Creeping Up On You is very good, but it's also very gross; one you may be tempted to fast forward through on repeat viewings. Well, I particularly dislike cockroaches...<br /><br />This film is loaded with familiar faces, creepy thrills, and even lots of humor, and it's well worth having in your collection. The DVD is a bit lackluster, but at least it is widescreen (as well as full) and comes with the theatrical trailer. Say what you want, but a lot of great films don't even get that much! Of course, for the Blu-Ray release, there ought to be more, or at least an improved transfer. But, I HEAR it appears exactly the same as the DVD! So, basically, you should go with whichever you find more affordable. They are, as I've heard, basically the same thing. Myself, I've had the DVD for a long time. It was probably one of my first DVDs, in fact, probably because I DID find it one of the more affordable DVDs at the time I was getting into the format. At any rate, love the film and thoroughly enjoy rewatching it, especially at this time of year. I highly recommend it.ls, and even lots of humor, and it's well worth having in your collection. The band is in much of a pastoral mood throughout the release, and there was still a lot of work to be done. But this is still a good album. Looking For Someone begins the classic sound: Peter's resonant soulful voice, Tony Banks' powerful keyboard playing, and a dark sinister feel. B001ILFUDC|3.0|Pinocchio (1940 Disney) was a \\"message movie\\" for adults as well as children.<br /><br />Pinocchio (1940 Disney) starring Dickie Jones, Cliff Edwards, Walter Catlett, and Frankie Darro (voiceover talent for major cartoon characters in the movie) is a satisfying, simple, well done movie set in the Swiss Italian mountains of northern Italy, and is based on an Italian folk tale by \\"Collodi.\\" It was Walt Disney's second feature length cartoon following the epic success of Snow White And The Seven Dwarfs (1937 Disney), which made more money than any in movie history up to that time (and this was the Golden Age Of Big Studio Hollywood) and which lit the fire under the MGM studios to create The Wizard Of Oz (1939 MGM) starring Judy Garland to cash in on the same unexpected and spectacular success Disney's Snow White movie had achieved.<br /><br />Pinocchio (1940 Disney) was a simpler movie, not set in a grand palace or peopled by elegant royalty or ending with a final reward for the main character of being taken by Prince Charming to a golden castle in the sky with celestial choirs grandly singing \\"Someday My Prince Will Come\\" punctuated by sonorous wedding bells. The audience is not expected to cheer for victorious and righteous royalty at the conclusion of Pinocchio (1940 Disney).<br /><br />It's a story about a lonely bachelor artist and craftsman, Gepetto, who makes toys and entertaining clocks which \\"perform\\" mechanically.<br /><br />Gepetto creates a marionette type puppet in the shape of a little boy, and wishes in his prayers the boy would come alive and become real, thus making Gepetto a father. After he prays and goes to sleep, his home is visited by \\"The Blue Fairy\\" who grants half of Geppetto's wish as a reward for the old man's goodness and kindness. The puppet comes alive, but is still made of wood, and will only achieve status as a \\"real boy\\" (i.e. a human boy with real flesh and bones) if the puppet earns that by doing good things, and listening to his conscience. A visiting cricket named \\"Jiminey\\" is recruited for the job, and after Gepetto awakens and learns to his joy that Pinocchio has come alive, the puppet goes out into the world where he must make good by doing good, and in the end, after several set backs, does that. He rescues his puppet maker father from a giant whale named \\"Monstro.\\"<br /><br />The movie ends happily with no departure from the happy old man's small home which celebrates the end of his loneliness, and the arrival of human company in his life. Jiminey Cricket ends the movie (the incredible, lilting, haunting voice of \\"Ukelele Ike\\" Cliff Edwards, the voice of \\"Jiminey Cricket\\") with the simple song \\"When You Wish Upon A Star\\" and a final portrait of the lovely star lit night time sky casting it's friendly light over the small Swiss Italian village. A simple, beautiful end to a great movie.<br /><br />Before the main character, Pinocchio, achieves success, he has two set backs. Both are set in the world of entertainment.<br /><br />He becomes a success as an actor, a star. But Pinocchio's actor success is not rewarded. Instead, his producer, Stromboli, locks the new actor star in a bird cage, and Pinocchio becomes a miserable prisoner beholding to the big money bosses who profit from his work (a realistic comment on the reality of show business.)<br /><br />The entire movie is great entertainment, often touching, and filled with worthwhile and humane messages about the human condition, it's follies, and it's ultimate (at times) nobility.<br /><br />Pinocchio (1940 Disney) is a wonderful movie worth screening often, as great in a proletarian way as Snow White And The Seven Dwarfs (1937 Disney) was great at portraying the world of story book royals, both good and bad.<br /><br />--------------<br /><br />Written by Tex Allen, SAG Actor.cience. 1400032717|1.0|In The Curious Incident, Haddon takes a good idea and mangles it so profoundly that despite me wanting to like this book, I have very little nice to say about it. The Curious Incident is a first-person narrative that tells the story of Christopher, a developmentally challenged boy who takes it upon himself to solve the mystery of the murder of the neighbour's poodle. There's no denying that my opinion of this book is heavily influenced by my experience with my own brother, who has autism and other developmental disabilities. Admittedly, Haddon does provide some fascinating theoretical insights into the thought-process of his narrator. However, the different way of thinking seems to define Christopher. There are no redeeming qualities to this character. It is as though he is incapable of experiencing kindness or love but only goes through the motions of determination and fear. Christopher is essentially robbed of his humanity and his mystery condition becomes the character by itself. In a landscape of an underdeveloped genre of literature, Haddon seems to create some unfair perceptions and conceptions of people living with autism or similar disorders. His thinly-veiled social commentary delivered by his narrator also detracts from Christopher ever becoming his own fully-developed character. I'm not going to say this book should not be read, but if it is, the reader should at all times treat this work as complete and pure fiction with little foundation in reality.

0375507256|3.0|It took only a few pages to realize this book is written in the postmodern style I've yet failed to appreciate. As a group, these novels are cynical, overly erudite in places, with too much attention to the story's voice and not enough to the remaining characteristics of good novels, i.e., sharp dialogue, tension lessening scenic depictions and  broad narratives. And Cloud Atlas fits perfectly in the bullseye of such a description.<br /><br />It's a difficult plot to summarize, but I'll try - in a hundred words or less. The book begins and ends in an epistolic account of the relations of South Sea islanders and whites in a particular century of European colonization. Then there's more letter-writing, between composer Robert Frobisher to a friend, Sixsmith, concerning RF's 1930s alliance with a famed Belgian composer Vyvyan Ayrs and his family, followed by some seesawing time changes in which big corporations are hiding their screw-ups, even as they're screwing over the people of the planet. This begins a slow slide into apocalypse in which tribal life returns, but with some vestige of technology. Shades of Plato's Republic!<br /><br />First, I'll be nice and say I admire Mitchell's imagination, his vision for such a monumental work. Clearly, much thought has been put into this work. Each of the several segments contains excitement, suspense, and many elements of a good mystery. Kudos for that.<br />But his dialogue, while sizzling in places, seems a one-note samba emotionally, and in one section in which he resorts to a made-up dialect, it's downright distracting. His various narrators' voices overwhelm any stabs he might have made at setting and complementary moods, and overall the text is a sprawling affair that often invites boredom. If you haven't read the book but plan to, my advice is to read fast. Either that or tie a flotation device to your ass to save you from drowning in the book's pretentious depths. Okay, that's pretty snotty - I apologize.<br /><br />Still the book is worth reading. Mitchell has a grasp of many of the implications of modern times' so-called progress. As he writes near story's end, \\"...one fine day, a purely predatory world shall consume itself...Is this the doom written within our nature?\\" Surely this question must be asked, over and over, in as many ways as possible, as Mitchell tries to do, if the human experiment is to be worth its existence.till the book is worth reading. B009AMAM9S|4.0|Trying to make some movies, big movies, work is like trying to catch a whale with a rod and reel. It's not easy to do and herein lies the problem with \\"Cloud Atlas.\\"<br /><br />Here's the scoop. The viewer is taken through several stories, spanning several centuries, that show how actions at one time can effect things further in the future.<br /><br />To say this is ambitious in both concept and scope is an understatement. At a nearly three hour running time it took both the Wachowskis and Tow Twyker to direct, with four writers and a cadre of producers. There were even multiple set and costume designers. The effects budget alone could run a small country for a year. But, as much as I liked it, and as much as there was to like, it lacked emotional punch. At the end, given the content of the stories, I should have been emotionally strung out but I wasn't. In one story, a female looking android, discovers the secret that old androids are processed to feed current androids (there is a nod to \\"Soylnet Green\\") and she somehow becomes a martyr and near deity for centuries to come. I didn't feel a thing. This was true for all but possibly one of the stories.<br /><br />I haven't read the source material by Mitchell, which many seem to like a lot, so I can't comment on the faithfulness the movie shows to the book. But again, this is a sweeping epic with a lot going for it. It's a visual as well as conceptual feast. Scene after scene will burn itself in the viewers memory. It was a bit distracting to try and peal away the makeup to see who was playing which role, and some of that makeup was pretty awful, but that's a minor quibble given everything. I will probably add it to my library upon it's Blu-Ray release, but it's just that bit short of being a truly great film. Do see it. Films of this magnitude are few and far between. Just be prepared for a mental workout and an emotional letdown.

1594744769|3.0|Jacob grew up listening to his grandfather Abe's stories of fantastical and harrowing adventures, most stemming from the orphanage he grew up in - Miss Peregrine's Home for Peculiar Children. How peculiar exactly are these children? Just judging from the photos on the covers - things get really weird.<br /><br />  I had seen this book at the bookstore several times, and I was always creeped out by the photos and the premise doesn't give much away. It wasn't until I actually bought the book and really looked at it that I noticed the girl on the cover is levitating, it just made me all that more excited to find out what was going on.<br /><br />  Our main character Jacob loved his grandfather Abe and his stories about the war, and the unique children he befriended at the orphanage he grew up in with their unusual abilities. But like any child, he got older and became increasingly skeptical about how real these stories could be. It's incredible how we rationalize everything, even when there's real physical photographic proof in front of us and Jacob is no different. But when his grandfather dies in a horrific accident, Jacob starts to question his sanity while plagued with nightmares and his grandfather's last request. Piecing the fragments of his grandfather's life together, Jacob ends up on a remote isle off the coast of Wales to search for answers - but he soon gets sucked into a world full of wonders and dangers he couldn't even begin to imagine.<br /><br />  Grandfather Abe completely reminds me of Grandpa Abe from The Simpsons. He tells war stories, and each time the version changes a little bit and it gets wilder and couldn't possibly be true. Soon relatives start brushing him off and thinking he's not that mentally stable anymore. But boy are you in for a surprise when the truth about Abe's life is uncovered and how hard he tried to the very end to fend off the things that go bump in the night.<br /><br />  This book is definitely a work of art; from the eerie photos to the end papers, to the in between chapter papers and the handwritten letters - it's all gorgeous and makes this an enveloping and engaging read. Although I don't think I'll ever be able to look at the photos for more than a few seconds - it feels like their unblinking eyes are just boring into your soul, even after we get to know some of these peculiar children.<br /><br />  The story immediately draws you in with the photos, and Abe's unfortunate end, but then it lulls a bit as Jacob struggles to figure things out. But Riggs does an excellent job contrasting Jacob's life before and after these life altering events so that when he does find the orphanage he easily fits in and it just feels right that he's there. However, for me this is where the story shifted gears completely. It was no longer a creepy mystery and I just didn't feel as invested in the story as I did before. It turns into a superheroes unite to save the world kind of situation and while I still wanted to know what happens, the last half of the story ends up as a predictable set up for the sequel and left a lot of questions unanswered.<br /><br />This story definitely has a unique and intriguing plot bolstered by old fashioned photos (and apparently they're all real?) and I generally enjoyed it. Which is true - the main piece of the set folds up and slides right back down into the box.  But set upand breakdown is time consuming, especially the first time; trust me, you do not want to rush it.  The instructions aren't great.  They'd be a lot more helpful and easier to read if they were in color.  It didn't help that my set was damaged when it arrived.  One corner of the main piece was bent in enough that you could see the stress to the plastic, and enough that unfolding it for the first time was difficult.  Additionally, some of the pieces (like the desks and tables) probably weren't meant to be taken apart and reassembled multiple times.  So if you don't have space to store those items intact once they're put together, be very careful in taking them apart and putting them back together later.  Trust me on this - as I was taking apart one of the desks when I was ready to put the set away, the end of one of the bars popped off.  A spot of glue should fix it, and the bit is more for looks I think, but still disappointing.<br /><br />* The furniture is really neat.  But the dolls don't want to sit at the desks or benches without some maneuvering. You can see what I mean in the photo of Operetta and Jackson in the cafeteria above; the dolls are posed with their legs crossed way out in front of them, to hold them steady.  You should also be aware going in that you will need your dolls' stands if you want to pose them at the DJ stand or at the lockers.<br />Not an issue for me, but for some parents, will be the number of small parts.  The little pens and the locks are really tiny so if you have small children or pets in your home, please use extra caution.  Also, I remember how sad I would be as a little girl when I would lose the small pieces or shoes that came with my dolls.  It's a good idea to save the little plastic sacks that the bits come in to keep them together when it's time to put the set away unless you already have an organization/storage system for bits like this.<br /><br />Is it worth the price? I have seen it at other retailers for over eighty dollars, which I don't think is reasonable at all.<br /><br />Despite the few issues I had, I think this is a great set. But instead of handing over your $$ in order to line Amanda Byrne's pocketbook... please, please, please instead check this book out for free from a library, or borrow a copy from a friend, or locate some free passages online.  Stick it to her!!  Now, for starters, this particular paragraph just rubbed me completely raw:  &#34;Whether we realize it or not, we are thinking most of the time. If you are speaking or listening to someone, you are thinking. If you are reading the newspaper or watching television, you are thinking. When you recall memories from your past, you are thinking. When you are considering something in your future, you are thinking. When you are driving, you are thinking. When you are getting ready in the morning, you are thinking.&#34;  Gee, I'm not sure I get the point... Do you think perhaps she might be trying to tell me that I am, ummmm, CONSTANTLY THINKING?  And then there's this:  &#34;You have two sets of feelings:  good feelings and bad feelings.  And you know the difference between the two because one makes you feel good, and the other makes you feel bad.&#34;    {start sarcasmn} Whew! I am so much more enlightened now after having read that little nugget.  I finally have labels for my contrasting feelings... and now I know how to tell them apart!  How did I ever survive before realizing this? {end sarcasm}  And to think, such &#34;wisdom&#34; keeps popping up ad nauseam throughout the book... An hour-long concert of fingernails scratching across a chalkboard is much more pleasurable to sit through than reading this kind of crap.  I don't know what is more insulting... the actual content of the book or the fact that it was even green-lighted to begin with. And Oprah!  Dear beloved Oprah!  How COULD you endorse a book that preaches, &#34;If you see people who are overweight do not observe them but immediately switch your mind to the picture of you in your perfect body and feel it.&#34;  Selling this line of thinking to the masses has just as many frightening implications as the trying to sell the following: &#34;If you see people who are Jewish do not observe them but immediately switch your mind to the picture of you in your perfect race and feel it.&#34;  (And we all know that the &#34;mental&#34; snuffing of Jews didn't end at just that....)  If you might be thinking, &#34;Well, that's got to be an exaggerated comparison, Rhonda Byrne is not implying the genocide of fat people&#34;, well... think again.  Rhonda Byrne may not be advocating physical genocide but she certainly is advocating the mental kind, encouraging her readers to embrace the US vs THEM mentality. &#34;Us&#34; being the thinner, the richer, the superior (or those who strive to attain such shallow niceties) and &#34;Them&#34; being anyone who is, well... not &#34;Us&#34;.  A person who tries to find their identity through such a skewed mentality can become so desensitized to &#34;them&#34; that violence against &#34;them&#34; often can and does erupt, whether it is the murder of one..... or 6 million.  And to think, regarding &#34;events in history where masses of lives were lost,&#34;  Rhonda Byrne has the AUDACITY to say, &#34;By the law of attraction, they had to be on the same frequency as the event.&#34;  So, according to this &#34;law&#34; of attraction, Holocaust victims essentially &#34;thought&#34; the violence upon themselves?  ! ! ! ! ! !  (The exclamation points are a polite substitution for what I was REALLY thinking... I have to keep my review G-rated, of course.)  Amanda Byrne must have never read a book such as Holocaust survivor Elie Wisel's &#34;Night&#34;.Or if she has, she probably did not &#34;observe&#34; any of Wisel's horrific written accounts, and instead chose to envision her own &#34;perfect&#34; book and &#34;feel&#34; all the millions of dollars she would rake in by selling it to the gullible masses!!!  But I digress...  A quote at the beginning of the paragraph about weight reads: &#34;Make it your intention to look for, admire, and inwardly praise people with your idea of perfect-weight bodies.&#34;  Are you effing kidding me?  With shallow advice like that, is it any wonder, then, that people (mainly women and young girls) end up obsessing over their own bodies and develop eating disorders?  And I can't even BEGIN to tell you how completely turned off I was when I read THIS:  &#34;Everything that surrounds you right now in your life, including the things you're complaining about, you've attracted. Now I know at first blush that's going to be something that you hate to hear...and I'm here to be a little bit in your face and to say, yes you did attract it.&#34;  Ok, I'm sorry, but if you feel you have to &#34;get in my face&#34; to make me believe what you are telling me, well, then you're insulting my intelligence.  REAL experts in REAL sciences don't have to get in anybody's face to convince them of a REAL law.  (I've never heard of a single physicist/scientist/academic professor ever having to get in anyone's face to prove the law of gravity.  Have you?)  And besides, no matter what kind of point someone is trying to make, whether it be about this so-called &#34;law&#34; or about something as ordinary as an opinion about a new movie, if it is coupled with an &#34;in-your-face&#34; attitude, I'm not going to take to the message - even if it is something I might happen to agree with - very kindly.  That whole &#34;in-your-face&#34; statement comes across as condescending and pompous.  And just a little further on down in that section:  &#34;Nothing can come into your experience unless you summon it [all that is negative] through persistent thoughts.&#34;  Really?  So the pizza-delivery guy who gets car-jacked at 1:00 am in the morning, robbed at gunpoint, then gets shot and killed actually summoned the thugs to appear out of the dark right at that moment?  Now, the problem with &#34;The Secret&#34; is that the quotes I referenced above are not the only disturbing/damaging/insulting ones to be found.  The book is FULL of them.  Don't believe me?  Well, all you have to do is read the book to find out for yourself.  Last, but certainly not least, the only logical explanations I can come up with for the baffling number of 5-star reviews for &#34;The Secret&#34; are:  1. Reviewers actually wrote a negative review but accidentally selected 5 stars (if you read some of the 5-star reviews, you'll realize it's clear that some people really did make this mistake...)  2. Shameless investors/promoters of this book have inflated the number of 5-star reviews by writing bogus posts.  3. Real people are writing about their real experiences with positive thinking, but mistakenly attribute the positive things happening in their lives to having read and espoused &#34;The Secret&#34;, as opposed to the fact that they might have just had a stroke of plain luck, or, even better yet - they actually worked hard for what they got. 1439156816|5.0|I consciously avoided reading \\"On Writing\\" for years. I finally broke down and acquired a copy of it and I'm glad I did.<br /><br />Part memoir and part how-to, \\"On Writing\\" is one of the best books on writing that I've ever read. In fact, I'd put it in the top 3 books on writing that I've read. Maybe even top 2, or even the coveted #1 spot. It's really good.<br /><br />King manages to use his tragic accident as motivation to pen one of the most useful books on writing ever. He doesn't issue the normal salvos or drop cliches like you typically find in books on writing. His explanations are terse and he uses examples from his own writing habits as well as the writing habits of other writers. Instead of giving you a nuts-and-bolts how-to instruction manual, he instead approaches the task of writing in a practical and creative way, explaining the bare bones for a novice writer while leaving specifics about his recommendations to the individual writer's imagination, style, and comfort zone. What a breath of fresh air!

B0098460O4|4.0|I won't trash this album with a low rating because Jonathan and Charlotte deserve all the success they can get.  But the arrangements and the mixing is bad.  Not just bad by professional standards, but bad by amateur standards.  First rule: never step on the vocals.  Cowell and crew didn't exactly step on them.  They drove over them in something slightly larger than an Abrams tank.  I thought that what was done to Susan Boyle in her first albums was bad, but this is worse.  I can't imagine listening to this mess and thinking it sounds just fine, although having a tin ear might make that possible.  If you don't have one, borrow one of Simon Cowell's.<br /><br />Is all lost?  Not exactly.  If you're listening to the album on a computer, download Winamp (if you don't have it) and turn on the equalizer.  It's just a ten band, but it will help.  Cut the 60hz slider the full 12db and the 170hz slider about 7db, maybe more.  That eliminates much of the low frequency droning that ruins many cuts.  I found that cutting the 14khz slider by 4db and the 16khz slider by 7db helped the sound, but that may be personal preference.  If you're listening with headphones, realize that although the headphones may have a flat frequency response, there is an ear canal resonance causing a peak at about 7khz.  You might experiment with the 6khz slider to see if that smooths the sound.  All this does nothing to eliminate the damned choir, but it does make the album listenable and in many places, very enjoyable.  After all, these kids are really good.<br /><br />If you're listening on a surround sound system, you have better options.  Charlotte and Jonathan appear to both be dead center, even when doing a duet.  They will be on the center channel, so all you have to do is cut the volume on the right and left channels (maybe the surrounds as well; I'm a two channel guy).  That should do an even better job of correcting the botched job done by the \\"pros.\\"  Alas, it sounds as if the aforementioned damned choir is also centered.<br /><br />It would be interesting to know J&C's reaction the first time they heard the finished(?) album.  After all, the accompanying noise tracks are laid down after the vocals, and the singers don't know what's about to hit them.  I'm betting tears were shed.

Isaac Newton, Albert Einstein, Kurt G&ouml;del, Immanuel Kant, and many others were mystics before they were  philosophers, physicists, or mathematicians. The book elaborates the notion that we progress in three stages, bringing order out of the cosmos and chaos that confronts us: poesy - philosophy - physics. Each stage follows from the previous, sometimes immediately, sometimes in halting succession, stretched out over time, often spanning generations. The `order from chaos' process is cyclic. 0307960471|5.0|Jo Nesb's \\"Phantom,\\" expertly translated by Don Bartlett, brings Harry Hole back to Norway after a three-year stay in Hong Kong.  He has returned because eighteen-year-old Oleg Fauke, son of his former lover, Rakel, is in prison, after allegedly gunning down a friend and fellow drug addict, nineteen-year-old Gusto Hanssen.  Harry familiarizes himself with Oleg's case and Oslo's squalid drug scene.  The financial stakes have soared since a synthetic and highly addictive substance called \\"violin\\" displaced conventional heroin as the drug of choice.  It is purer, less toxic, and more expensive than its predecessor.<br /><br />\\"Phantom\\" features a more mature Harry Hole.  Our hero used to be a self-destructive drunk, but he is now a recovering alcoholic and addict, trying to face life with clarity and sobriety.  He is still a gifted and tenacious investigator who methodically takes in every aspect of a crime scene, noticing minutiae that others miss.  Since he is no longer employed as a detective, Harry relies on friends and acquaintances for help.  Most of them oblige--some willingly, others reluctantly.<br /><br />This story is, in some ways, reminiscent of \\"Rashomon,\\" the classic film about human frailty, the subjectivity of perception, and the elusiveness of truth.  In this case, we are like members of a jury who listen to conflicting testimony from a variety of sources.  In italicized passages, Gusto, the dying junkie, poignantly and heartbreakingly bares his soul as his life drains away.  He is paying the ultimate price for taking and selling drugs \\"that consume the soul, cause the body to disintegrate and send the buyer into dependency's cold gripping hell.\\"<br /><br />In addition, we meet a cast of venal, power-hungry, corrupt, arrogant, deceitful, and violent people, most of whom would willingly barter their souls for profit and influence.  Not everyone is a scoundrel; there are a few decent and honest individuals who are both compassionate and altruistic. Harry Hole is a force of nature who manages to offend, bully, and cajole his way into an investigation that is, technically, none of his business.  He pays a steep emotional and physical price for his involvement.  Readers will be open-mouthed at the physical abuse that Harry endures in the course of this novel.  Most people who are battered, bruised, and bleeding would consult a doctor or at least try to get their hands on some potent antibiotics.  \\"Superman\\" Harry grits his teeth, treats his wounds with sewing thread and duct tape, and proceeds with his inquiries.<br /><br />The author's vibrant figurative language, cutting and sarcastic dialogue, scathing social commentary, and ingeniously convoluted plot keep us riveted. In addition, Nesb provides valuable insight into his characters' complex motives.  Red herrings abound, startling developments keep us off balance, and the conclusion is a shocker.  \\"Phantom\\" is an engrossing police procedural that, for all its bleakness, black humor, and unrelenting violence, underscores Harry's selfless devotion to Oleg and Rakel, for whom he unquestioningly risks his life.rry Hole is a force of nature who manages to offend, bully, and cajole his way into an investigation that is, technically, none of his business. B000002UAC|5.0|Seriously, people really need to buy a clue before they write such uneducated reviews on Amazon. I see 2 or less stars in the ratings for this album stating it's not up to the standard of other seminal rock groups - Stones, Zeppelin, Purple, Who, Kinks etc.<br /><br />Let's establish something here, namely when this album was recorded  - August 1963  :  The Stones had only just been in the studio to record their debut single 'Come On'(and a Chuck Berry cover at that) for Decca. The Glimmer Twins had not yet been locked in that room by Andrew Loog Oldham to come out with something original that was decent. Yes folks, THIS was the album that convinced Oldham that songwriting by the band themselves was the way of the future. Covers were not sustainable, and he was correct in that assumption. Little Richie Blackmore was in an instrumental band dancing lame steps - a la Shadows - ina low budget film with The Outlaws (with Chas Hodges later from Chas & Dave fame. Search youtube \\"The Outlaws - Law & Order\\" to see). Jimmy Page was a session guitarist in London playing on \\"horrific Elevator music\\". The mighty Led Zeppelin were not even a twinkle in his eye yet. The Who were still the High Numbers and The Kinks had no record contract.<br /><br />So with this in mind we can do an historical evaluation to see where Rock & Pop were at at this time. ROCK as we know it had not yet happened and POP as we know was being ushered in by, and only by  -  The Beatles.  Yes this album WITH THE BEATLES laid the platform and inspiration for bands to follow.<br /><br />No, The Beatles were not the reason some these classic bands formed - they were around way before influenced by the Rhythm & Blues greats and early Rockers. But it was this album that established Lennon & McCartney as real songwriters (aeolian cadences, the greatest composers since Schubert was being thrown around after this album smashed it's way into public consciousness) and inspired the soon-to-be Rock greats to mold theirown identities and not be mere cover band acts. Merely 3 years later, John & Paul were unanimously the greatest songwriters unrivaled by anybody in modern music.<br /><br />There are some great Lennon-McCartney tracks here  : It Wont Be Long, All I've Got To Do, Not A Second Time, All My Loving, Little Child. George Harrison bats very fair with his songwriting debut 'Dont Bother Me'. Throw in the covers 'Mr Postman, You Really Got A Hold On Me, Roll Over Beethoven' and the amazing version of the R&B classic  'Money' - the ultimate version that John Lennon turned into a piece of primal therapy, seven years before Arthur Janov - and here you have an album light years ahead of any band of the era. This was the album that had young kids in Britain wanting to buy guitars and try their luck at the big time and be Beatles. This was the album that America fell in love with 6 months later, though it was titled 'Meet The Beatles' and was embellished by the soon to be recorded  I Want To Hold Your Hand, This Boy and a few from Please, Please Me.<br /><br />'Please, Please Me' was a tremendous album debut and showed undoubted promise but it was still 1950's in approach and style that did hint at something new , fresh and original. WITH THE BEATLES delivers on that promise and changed the course of modern music. I dare say culture.<br /><br />Of course The Beatles bested themselves with every release ie. every release was a progression from the last and they would soon best this with Hard Day's Night. No band under their schedule and pressure has ever came close to the work they created in a mere 7 years recording.

1451695195|5.0|Great book - but lots of questions! If true, this is perhaps the most astounding, important, enlightening and uplifting story ever told. It complements, supplements and/or trumps all spiritual revelations and materialist musings up to this point in human history. If people think about this correctly (and I have little hope that the majority will), and if it can be confirmed, we would all come together and live happily ever after; this is the stuff of fairy tale; in the good sense.<br /><br />Is it real? Certainly for all intents and purposes, Dr. Alexander is highly credible. So either: 1) it is true, or 2) he is somehow misinterpreting a really strange hallucination caused by brain damage as reality or 3) he is making it all up. Personally I discount this last option.<br /><br />Is it possible that this was a strange hallucination caused by brain damage? Dr. Alexander is sure that it is not. The fact that he received validation with the photo of his sister at the end is really stunning! But I suppose it is possible that his mind (or brain) may have been playing tricks on him. A critical question for me is whether or not this journey actually did occur when his neo-cortex was non-functional as he says as oppose to being in a degraded state. He says he has time anchors which demonstrate that the majority of the really sublime experience did in fact occur when his neo-cortex was non-functional. If this journey really did occur when his neo-cortex was non-functional, then clearly, it would seem, consciousness is not reducible to material causation as materialism requires. However, even if there is some doubt about when the most exquisite elements of his journey occurred, the following very difficult question remains for those who adopt a materialist line of thinking: How can it be imagined that such advanced concepts discloses in the book which rang very true to me (there are more that are difficult to articulate) could be evoked out of a severely compromised brain?<br/><br />If this is real then virtually everything that nearly all the scientists in all the universities of all the world have been saying for decades about evolution, the brain and mind is spectacularly wrong! If there is a conscious mind apart from the brain then it must have been bestowed by some pre-existent intelligent entity--probably some divine entity. But materialist scientists are not going to like the idea that they themselves and the scientific paradigm itself could be regarded as fallible any more than the authority of the leaders of the church was challenged a few centuries ago. If this story gets traction, materialist scientists are going to wage war on Dr. Alexander.<br /><br />A few thoughts about the phenomenon of consciousness related to Dr. Alexander's experience. I have never understood how intelligent people--including Dr. Alexander until his NDE--could dismiss consciousness as a phenomenon of the physical brain. I have never accepted that for a wide variety of reason only one of which I will mention: If human consciousness were reducible to physical phenomenon then what accounts for the immutability of one's sense of self? How can it be imagined that, in Dr. Alexander's case, after the brain damage he incurred and once his neo-cortex was restored (miraculously), that his sense of self--that he is the same Eben Alexander he was before--be perfectly reconstituted? How can a physical algorithm--organic computer--account for that especially in light of the fact that his brain structure must have undergone catastrophic change? Personhood--self, identity whatever you want to call it--is constant despite radical changes in the underlying physical structure. In fact, how can trillions of firing neurons account for consciousness at all? When the best explanation offered by materialist to explain consciousness--the most real thing about us--is that it is an illusion, you know they are really grasping at straws.<br /><br />I am anxious to read more about Dr. Alexander's experience. He has written 20,000 words about it.

When the main arguments of the book do arrive, they turn out to be based on somewhat preliminary and speculative physics- very interesting, but nowhere near satisfying or convincing as an explanation of 'how something could arise from nothing.' Also, the author promises to show how the universe(s) could come into existence without 'preexisting' physical laws- his nothing ne plus ultra- but fails to actually do so. Honestly I would have been quite surprised and impressed if he had; but it illustrates the most frustrating aspect of the book, which is that it purports to sketch out a framework to obviate all manner of prime mover / first cause arguments, but fails pretty resoundingly to do so.<br /><br />Still, thought provoking and well worth reading.

From there, the comedy of errors gets funnier, and gorier, as the preps and assorted helpers from local law enforcement keep trying to rescue Allison--who's actually having quite a nice time bonding with Dale and playing board games--and running into the former occupant's traps or stumbling, sometimes quite literally, into fatal predicaments.<br /><br />There's not much I can say about this other than that it succeeds on all counts, and it does so in the most smashing of ways. You will get yourself sucked into a hole, one as deep and cavernous as the Silo, keeping you trapped until you finish and crawl back into reality, blinking away tears at the sudden light. Get ready for an awesome ride.<br /><br />Synopsis:<br />A society of people living in an underground silo, their only view of the uninhabitable planet through cameras on the outside. Most residents live their lives out, working their jobs, getting married, hoping to be chosen in the lottery to be able to bear children, without ever upsetting the status quo. However, a unintentionally scavenged piece of knowledge that results in an  always-deadly \\"Cleaning\\" of the cameras outside, sets off a sequence of events that will end up affecting every person in the Silo.<br /><br />The Awesome:<br />Character development truly carries this series. Howey is skilled at making these people utterly real and relatable, so much that the pain they feel, you'll end up experiencing as well. I love, love, LOVE that we have a female main character that is not a killer sexpot, innocent sexpot, or psycho sexpot. Juliette is strong, intelligent, and principled, and engineer to the bone. There desperately needs to be more characters like Jules, to inspire the next generation of engineers and mechanically-inclined women.<br /><br />Setting is superb. Evocative enough that as the book progresses, you start to experience the silo on a sensory level, feeling the steps underneath your feet, the perpetually recycled air against your skin. However, it is done subtly, without making you slog through pages or even just paragraphs of description. It's seamlessly interwoven with plot, as it should be, adding depth to the story rather than distracting from it.<br /><br />Plot is driven, tightly woven, and does not disappoint. Even as you piece things together, you'll be almost frantic to discover what's going to happen, up to the very last chapter. Howey has no problem killing off a character he's made you care about, so keep tissues handy.<br /><br />The Not-So-Awesome:<br />It ends. :( It starts this insatiable hunger to know MORE, what HAPPENS, and then it has the temerity to go and END on us?! What the H! >:(

B001E1GXPA|5.0|When this CD came out, I was very hesitant to buy it. I had listened to Trivium's first three albums and, despite my fondness for progressive metal, was not particularly impressed; many of those songs sounded too rough (aesthetically under-polished) for my taste. But I read some pretty good reviews and figured I'd give it a try... and I was blown away. The progress and maturity that this band achieved in the mere two years since their previous release was astonishing. I enjoyed every single track on this album, especially Kirisute Gomen, Torn Between Scylla and Charybdis, and Shogun. The guitar work was much cleaner; the lyrics were more resonant, with deeper metaphors; and the vocals. Matt Heafy shines on every track of this album. His decision to use his screaming purposefully--as in Like Callisto, where the screamed vocals represent one character and the clean vocals another--sets the bar for the rest of the prog-metal community. And his baritone voice on the track Shogun is still the standard to which I hold many of my own vocals.<br />Now, four years later, this album is still my favorite of Trivium's LPs, and still one of my personal favorites. And if you don't already own them, the bonus tracks are worth purchasing.<br />I would recommend this CD, in particular, to fans of Metallica's Death Magnetic, Slipknot's Vol. 3: The Subliminal Verses, System of A Down's Toxicity, and Dream Theater's A Dramatic Turn of Events. Enjoy.

B008BSPQ3E|5.0|I'll leave it to others to provide a comprehensive review of the content of this box.  My review will focus on the remastered audio of the original album.<br /><br />As presented on the physical CD included in this set, the So album has become yet another victim in the ongoing \\"loudness wars\\" plaguing contemporary music releases.  What would otherwise be a good remastering has been rendered loud, limited and compressed, more so than on any previous release of the album.  And if that was where the story ended, I'd recommend this box set only for its other content, but would urge you to look elsewhere for a better copy of the album itself. However, when purchasing this set, you are also provided with the option of downloading the master 24-bit/48-kHz files, from which the CD itself was mastered.  (They can also be purchased for download separately.)  The mastering presented in these files is outstanding, and quite possibly surpasses all previous versions of this album.<br /><br />For this remaster, Peter Gabriel wanted to go back to the original sonic \\"vision\\" of the `80s master, and worked directly with the original mastering engineer for the album, Ian Cooper.  The result of their efforts is a sound quite similar to the `80s CD, but with higher resolution, a touch more warmth, and slightly more muscularity.  (Also, there is a tiny boost in loudness, and a touch of added compression, but nothing that really detracts from the overall listening experience.)  These qualities are all reflected in the 24-bit master files, because these are the masters Gabriel and Cooper created, prior to any other tinkering for other formats.  In other words, these files present the album tracks as Gabriel and Cooper intended them to be heard, and they sound superb.<br /><br />In contrast, the CD is the sound of a record company commercializing those results.  When used for the physical CD, the master files were subjected to additional compression and limiting, thereby substantially increasing loudness, reducing dynamic range, and causing a significant amount of clipping.  Comparing the waveforms of the two versions shows the 24-bit files to be well within their maximum range, with plenty of punch and breathing room for transients.  Conversely, the CD waveforms show amplitude signals to be maxed out all over the place, with clearly \\"shaved\\" peaks that have nowhere to go.<br /><br />These numbers show the maximum and average volume levels for \\"In Your Eyes\\" from the original 1986 CD, the 2002 CD remaster, the 2003 SACD remaster, the 2012 CD remaster, and the 2012 master file (units are percentage of a CD's maximum gain; 24-bit file converted to redbook for analysis):<br /><br />1986: Peak = 99.90; Average = 46.00<br /><br />2002: Peak = 99.47; Average = 67.25<br /><br />2003: Peak = 50.99; Average = 31.68 (normalizes to 61.51% average at 99% peak volume)<br /><br />2012 CD: Peak = 100; Average = 75.33<br /><br />2012 master file: Peak = 99.06; Average = 55.50<br /><br />To be fair, the levels of compression used on the new CD are not as egregious as on so many new releases these days, and overall the CD remains listenable.  Nevertheless, it presents an unneeded and detrimental departure from the mastering on the 24-bit files.  With these master files, Gabriel has given us the definitive sound of one of his greatest albums, and their availability is commendable.  It's just too bad their sound isn't faithfully preserved on the physical product included in this set.asing loudness, reducing dynamic range, and causing a significant amount of clipping.  Comparing the waveforms of the two versions shows the 24-bit files to be well within their maximum range, with plenty of punch and breathing room for transients.  Conversely, the CD waveforms show amplitude signals to be maxed out all over the place, with clearly \\"shaved\\" peaks that have nowhere to go.<br /><br />These numbers show the maximum and average volume levels for \\"In Your Eyes\\" from the original 1986 CD, the 2002 CD remaster, the 2003 SACD remaster, the 2012 CD remaster, and the 2012 master file (units are percentage of a CD's maximum gain; 24-bit file converted to redbook for analysis):<br /><br />1986: Peak = 99.90; Average = 46.00<br /><br />2002: Peak = 99.47; Average = 67.25<br /><br />2003: Peak = 50.99; Average = 31.68 (normalizes to 61.51% average at 99% peak volume)<br /><br />2012 CD: Peak = 100; Average = 75.33<br /><br />2012 master file: Peak = 99.06; Average = 55.50<br /><br />To be fair, the levels of compression used on the new CD are not as egregious as on so many new releases these days, and overall the CD remains listenable.  Nevertheless, it presents an unneeded and detrimental departure from the mastering on the 24-bit files.  With these master files, Gabriel has given us the definitive sound of one of his greatest albums, and their availability is commendable.  It's just too bad their sound isn't faithfully preserved on the physical product included in this set.

B008R9QAY6|5.0|Its finally here, Leonardo Vincis Artaserse, one of the most popular and influential operas of the 1730s. Leonardo Vinci was a hugely talented composer from Calabria, and though his birthplace and date of birth are still in dispute, he must have been born around 1696.<br />Vinci studied in the celebrated Conservatory dei Poveri di Ges Cristo in Naples, and began his career as an opera composer when he was in his early 20s. I first heard of him thanks to the marvelous recording of his comic opera Li Zitte NGalera with La Cappella deTurchini in the OPUS111 label. Earlier this year Dynamic released on cd a live recording of La Partenope also with La Cappella deTurchini, a marvelous opera whose libretto Handel later used for his own version of Partenope.<br />Now Virgin, and not a minute too soon, do him proud with a stellar recording of his last opera, Artaserse, to a libreto by Imperial Court Composer Pietro Metastasio. Artaserse became so popular and well liked that Charles de Brosses in Paris was referring to it as \\"the most celebrated Italian opera\\". Its really great music, with character, fantasy and endless melodic invention. Virgin has assembled a showcase of great countertenors, no less than 5, including Max Emanuel Cencic and Philippe Jaroussky. But the big aria of the opera that ends Act 1 \\"Vo solcando un mar crudele\\" goes to Franco Fagioli, a spectacular Argentinian countertenor with a Marilyn Horne technique,<br />But all the singing is absolutely spectacular (needless to say), and conductor Diego Fasolis left his excellent Swiss group I Barocchisti at home in favor of the legendary Concerto Kln, one of the best period orchestras around.<br />This is an extremely important recording because it finally makes available a much talked about masterpiece from the Baroque Era, and not only that, but a very enjoyable one too.<br />Leonardo Vinci like his equally talented contemporary Domenec Terradellas was murdered in Rome when he was still very young. There were talks of a love affair with a high born Roman lady, Vinci was reputedly very handsome, or more likely a jealous rival composer poisoned him (Porpora could hardly bear to hear his name), the fact is that the World was robbed of a major composer who lost his life at age 34.<br />This is infinitely better singing than what the MET can offer in Wagner these days, yes, Im talking of the new RING of course, and the Artaserse is an instant landmark recording. Do get it.

B0030Y11XS|1.0|From the very beginning, people like Michael Moore always attempt to redefine capitalism. Adam Smith already defined it hundreds of years ago. One of the pillars of that definition is the notion that people on both sides of a contract enter into it freely because they both believe they are getting something out of it. People like Michael Moore are all over the map on that one issue. On one hand, they really don't believe you're smart enough to make decisions for yourselves. On the other hand, they don't want business owners to be able to make their own decisions either. When they critique capitalism, they almost always criticize things that contain a heavy dosage of coercion, meaning the thing they are critiquing doesn't meet the most basic definition of capitalism.<br /><br />Michael Moore does not even really understand the thing he is attempting to criticize at the most basic level and it saddens me that people will view this movie without first understanding that when one side of a transaction is coerced, the transaction ceases to be capitalist in nature. 0375507256|4.0|CLOUD ATLAS (2004 Man Booker Prize Finalist) is complex fiction; David Mitchell joins (strings n weaves) six medium-length tales--each nearly sufficient unto itself and each passably resolved, together; notwithstanding the abrupt mid-sentence termination of Adam Ewing's journal entries at the outset (initial chapter) ah, intrigue in the offing (!) and despite how tenuously one character may suggest another--from a prior segment--Mitchell reintroduces them in a kind of second-half sequel, and so it all soon begins to work rather well.<br /><br />His characters (credible?--passably; likable?--two or three; where not--revelatory of gritty humanity) reflect each other across eras: 1850s, 1930s, 1970s, 1990s, and a science-fiction-era: Year 2144--in which cloning has led to an evolved yet enslaved sentient class) so it seems Mitchell is reincarnating his creations; also, there's this 'comet' tattoo of his that keeps popping up, tail n all (ooooh, so spooooky--nah, not really: the author neglects to drill this body-ink motif sufficiently to get it through my thick skull; never did say: \\"Ah, I get it!\\" As for Twilight Zone's theme wafting o're me whenever a story-connecting etude blew in, such woo-woos of his just did not resonate; but no matter, there's much to ponder:<br /><br />The characters' South Pacific, Belgian, China-cum-Korean and Hawaiian motifs kept my nose to the grindstone [page after page, small type] till I hit THE END. Why?--A COMPELLING QUALITY OF WRITING; each character, speaking in an era's dialect, did share (or came to share) a resigned cynicism (hey, there's little reason for optimism) yet also appear inclined to engage in and enhance life--no matter his or her limitations, or til life's tenebrous aspects overwhelm them, or that relentless killer shows up. CLOUD ATLAS is far more interesting than gloomy; it's just that life's deflating; did I mention mortal? Sad how oblivious Adam Ewing is to his own demise.<br /><br />A reader obstacle---for me, a nuisance---interpreting Mitchell's language style in `Sloosha's Crossin'--a 70-page segment that draws on Hawaiian folklore.<br /><br />[Warning: spoiler alert; the novel's line-up follows]<br /><br />  #1) Adam Ewing, notary---33-year old's journal entries in 1850s English are posted during a South Pacific three-masted sailing; Adam reports roguish behavior. (Hint: an avowed physician and a Christian missionary have much to answer for, but don't) Adam ends up owing his life to Autua, a freed slave, and becomes an abolitionist, determined to have a life worth living by helping to shape a world that he wants his son to inherit, and NOT one that he fears that his son will.<br /><br />  #2) Letters from Zedelghem: Musician/composer/amanuensis Robert Frobisher pursues an ex-pat Englander in failing health--a cantankerous composer: Vivyan Ayers; he stalks Ayers to a Belgian castle-hideaway on the outskirts of 1931 Bruges; Frobisher gains employment and lodging from the nearly-blind genius, and is seduced by Ayer's 50-ish wife, whose daughter Eva he ultimately lusts for (The Graduate? Nope. Things don't work out so well for Robert, as for Dustin); the talented young Englishman (Frobisher) seems compelled to unburden his woe-is-me rural existence in a series of letters to a mysterious `Sixsmith' -Frobisher's occasional benefactor, and, it comes to light, his once-upon-a-time lover. In the close, Frobisher has planned his suicide well; even so, he is doubtful that he will escape himself even in death; he believes \\"We do not stay dead long.\\" However, he employs the Luger, to see perhaps if his belief will stand up.<br /><br />  #3) Rufus Sixsmith unmasked:  in this 1970s noir segment he is 66 and an energy-consultant/nuclear scientist/whistle-blower in `Half-lives: The First Luisa Rey Mystery.' Its atomic-powered rending has a Three-Mile Island flavor; Mitchell ushers in murder-for-hire, corporate malfeasance, p.r.-intrigue, investigative journalism---whisking in comeuppancesfor all dem kats in the his cat n mouse fray. At one point the reader is left hanging, like when Luisa gets launched over a bridge-railing into a sure-watery death by hit-man Bill Smoke. But heroine Luisa survives such repeated close-calls, exposes corporate wrongdoing, and Smoke gets smoked.<br /><br />  #4) The Ghastly Ordeal of Timothy Cavendish was by far my favorite of the six tales: the protagonist (poor buggah suffers a stroke in Aurora House) is a n'er-do-well publisher/book-agent, also 66; he gets trapped in an assisted-living facility; his farcical attempt to escape the wretched place (and the clutches of Nurse Noakes--One Flew Over the Cuckoo's Nest sprang to mind) is humorous as all get out, if only he can get out; Looking at old-age homes: \\"You will not apply for membership, but the tribe of the elderly will claim you...this slippage will stretch your skin, sag your skeleton, erode your hair and memory, make your skin turn opaque so your twitching organs and blue-cheese veins will be semivisible...only babies, cats, and drug addicts will acknowledge your existence... and you will stand before a mirror, and think, E.T. locked in a ruddy cupboard.\\"  More: \\"Oh, once you've been initiated into the Elderly, the world doesn't want you back.\\" For laughs, re-read pages 360-361.  There's also the not-so-funny: \\"Unlimited power in the hands of limited people always leads to cruelty\\"--consider the right-wing dominated U.S. House of Representatives. It does turn out well for Mr. Cavendish; he relocates to a northern England village, his ordeal and middle-age left behind.<br /><br />  #5) AN ORISON OF SONMI, 451 -deposition of a cloned (fabricated) human, conducted by an archivist; her name: Sonmi; her production #: 451--assigned off the wombtank assembly line: a `perfect organic machinery' in a futuristic Nea So Copros regime. Having snuck herself an education, and uncovering a murderous government conspiracy, she's a major threat to establishment enforcers. She's awaiting execution, sees herself a martyr, and tells the skeptical archivist about show trials, and over-population solutions. Sonmi thus gives evidence of a grim 22nd century world: which is life under Papa Song--he may be the equivalent of The Great and Powerful Oz--a sham tyrant, made in this instance of bytes n bits. Two groups at odds: 1) Unanimity, whose Purebloods hold n wield power in a suffocating, corpocratic state, and 2) Union Abolitionists, whose aim it is to wrest power from Unanimity, and put an end to fabricant slavery. The setting? Northeast China, near a Korean peninsula. Sonmi tells the archivist that highly-ascended Purebloods (think Cheney, Rove, Rumsfeld, or the Koch bros.) intend to exterminate downscale Purebloods (anyone at all literate) in order to replace them with docile, readily controllable clones (say, ones beholden to Grover Norquist.)<br /><br />#6) Sloosha's Crossin: Zachry--this tale's illiterate narrator--and Meronym, an island guest, traverse the Big Isle of Hawaii together. Meronym (back from the future) stops in at Kona, ostensibly to study Island Life and to document how Hilo natives placate (toss in a few slaves every so often) their awesome god Mauna Kea; either He is a humongous volcano or He inhabits it. Who the slaves are depends on who's exercising dominance. This segment was my least favorite, its vernacular hard to fathom. Mitchell's witticisms, however, are numerous: \\"The nation-state is merely human nature inflated to monstrous proportions. Another war is always coming. War is one of humanity's two eternal companions.\\"<br /><br />Wrote this review in Feb 2011. Then in Oct 2012 I saw CLOUD ATLAS, the movie---dumbstruck the film project would even be tackled. Anyone who has NOT read the book, it's my belief, will be lost in this film, and likely frustrated. For the book's readers, the movie will be entertaining. B003W5C0VM|4.0|The film is a mix of good and bad.  The Stones are great, at the top of their game, which more than makes up for the horrible directing.<br /><br />The film starts with a great extended lead up to the Stones coming on stage.  Your anticipation builds until you hear the announcer say, \\"Ladies and Gentlemen the Rolling Stones\\".  You hear the crowd roar and you feel your hair stand on end.  Suddenly a shot of Charlie on the drums.  Then Keith ambles out in his typical style, followed by Mick Jagger bounding on to the stage.  More exciting anticipation, waiting for the band to start. These were the days before everything was timed and computerized and it took a minute or two for the band to get started once they got on stage.  Jagger walks back and forth a bit then mumbles into the microphone, something like, \\"Let's go, Keith\\".  Then Keith rips into \\"Brown Sugar\\", the crowd roars and you can just feel the excitement from the crowd.  You want to stand up and cheer watching it in your living room!  That lasts all of a fraction of the opening riff before the crowd sound is completely mixed out of the sound.  That's kind of it for the rest of the film.  No crowd.  I mean, this is a concert film.  Shouldn't we have some sense of the crowd?<br /><br />What follows is maybe the worst directed rock concert film of all time.  The camera shots are way too limited and usually too close up.  The sense of the size of the concert and event is lost in the viewing.  One great example -- for the song, \\"Happy\\", Keith sings lead, but the camera focuses on Mick Jagger, who is standing with his back to the audience waiting for Keith to sing.  The stage light is also on Mick as he frantically points toward Keith, signaling, \\"Over there!  I'm not singing this one, Keith is!\\"<br /><br />However, the Stones sound fantastic and are at their live concert peak.  Mick Taylor is a master on guitar throughout.  The rhythm section of the band, Keith, Bill and Charlie drive the songs with a steady intensity. He frequently turns back toward Charlie, focusing on keeping the rhythm of each song on track.  Great shot of Keith in the opening song, \\"Brown Sugar\\" as he plays in the background and grooves and lip syncs along with Mick.  Keith has rehearsed a great band for this tour and he is enjoying the product of his hard work.<br /><br />\\"All Down the Line\\" is my favorite performance of the film.  The band hits such a groove that you even catch the stoic Mick Taylor bopping his head a bit.  And no one seems to be enjoying the performance more than Mick Jagger himself, who at the end of the song appears to be dancing more to entertain himself than to please the crowd.  Other highlights of the show for me were \\"Brown Sugar\\" and any song with Mick and Keith together at the microphone (\\"Happy\\", \\"Dead Flowers\\").<br /><br />A must-have for Stones fans.  Yes, the film has its flaws, but no one is going to time travel back to 1972 and film us a better Stones concert.

It IS a work that will get one up and running with all of the most important aspects of the 256K Raspberry Pi. Note that the primary focus is on the revision B Pi (the one with the two USB ports). After a brief history of the Pi project, the user is given straightforward directions on hooking it up to monitors and keyboards. After simple directions on getting an operating system \\"flashed\\" on an SD card, you're off.<br /><br />By the end of this book, you will be shown the basics of Linux administration, mostly in the context of configuring the Pi and setting it up on a network. The rest of the book leads users to install home theater software, web servers, and several programming environments. In no way will you be an expert in any of these areas, but you WILL be up and running and ready to proceed a lot deeper in any direction of personal interest.<br /><br />Think of this book as the manual that comes with your new car. It's got every thing you need to get up and running, but if you want to tinker you'll need more information!
