B007PFD90G|4.0|I bought this because of a compelling excerpt in the New Yorker magazine. I have since spent nearly a month working through it, and here is the best response I can manage.<br /><br />Rushdie is a brilliant writer. As a prose stylist, he knows and employs every possible strategy of irony and comedy. He shifts well to fit the content he is describing. And he has much to say that is memorable. I have used my Kindle bookmark function extensively, more so than with any other book, to identify passages to which I wish to return, or which I wish to share with others. It is a book I will remember, and often wish to reference.<br /><br />But though he is brilliant, Rushdie also comes off here as a self-regarding, self-aggrandizing person who manages to emerge from a memoir (presumably written for people who consider his life of interest) looking excessively full of himself. I suppose that I should praise him for being honest enough about his thoughts and actions to allow this judgment to be available. So I will. Good job.<br /><br />No one reading this memoir could suppose Rushdie to be just plain folks. He is consistently a bon vivant (even under the fatwa), consistently wealthy, constantly flattered with honors and lectures and awards. People devote their lives and careers to defending his interests as he becomes a socio-cultural symbol. He gives lists of literati and occasional movie stars who are his dear friends, creating the impression that the restrictions imposed upon him by the fatwa were largely an inconvenience to his shining upper-crust lifestyle of dinner parties and drinking with nigh every famous writer of cultural note. This is the bulk of his narrative (though not the heart), and it has the effect of muting my ability either to pity Rushdie (which he does not ask for) or to admire him for standing up for free speech (which he might perhaps welcome, or which at least he is used to).<br /><br />I'm not saying Salman Rushdie is or was a bad man. So long as you are not married to him, he seems to be a loyal and loving friend. Clearly, he is a man of principle in regard to the arts, which is not the worst thing to be. And if so many people really do like him, there must be a reason. If I had access to the literati, and to giving lectures and such, I would live as he has lived. Who can blame him?<br /><br />To balance against my impression of him as a pompous, blathering philanderer (and he does express regret about his philandering), there is vast humor in this  book, emerging with brilliance from the murk of dinner parties, name-dropping, and posturing as bright ripe berries might emerge from a dung-colored bush. And wherever the folderol is not, there is meditation upon mortality, and there is touching depiction of his deep devotion to his family, including a moving, elegiac account, near the end, of the death of his first wife Clarissa. And there are accounts of the development of his literary ideas, although they are few and rare compared to the far less valuable accounts of his conviviality.<br /><br />The fault for this may well lie with Rushdie's editor. The editor should have confronted him with the textual imbalances and urged him to bolster those portions that deal best with his legacy. If Rushdie comes off as neurotic and self-centered in this book, as if trying both to lionize and to justify himself, then this may be because the focus of the book is defined by its page to page content, rather than its superb high points. Rushdie does acknowledge his mistakes as mistakes, and he does blame himself for the majority of them, and to his credit, he thanks people who should be thanked.<br />Perhaps the honesty of this representation is better, and perhaps there is a greater value in the oxymoronic depiction of a noble sybarite, than in the properly pitched version I have faulted the editor for failing to demand.  Perhaps it is the measure of a great memoir that it reflects and embodies the contradictions of its authoras this one does.as this one does.

1579126235|5.0|What gets me every time reading these detective murder mysteries is the amazing ability of the detective (Hercule Poirot in this case) to observe and make educated guesses about whether this or that happened; which clues are significant and which are not. The hero of these stories is in fact Poirot, not any of the characters. He is like Sherlock Holmes: he sees what other people do not see. He observes with 1000x magnification while others must be content with 2x. He logically deduces conclusions with agility, while others are left dumbfounded. In short, Poirot's reputation is well deserved. We, as readers instinctively root for him because we know that he will always get his man.<br /><br />Christie concentrates on character development. While Poirot is a familiar figure, the other characters are completely new to the reader. We have the villain Ratchett contrasted with the other passengers: Hardman, Col. Arbuthnot, Princess Dragonmiroff, Count Andrenyi, Countess Andrenyi, Mrs. Hubard, Greta Chisson, Mary Debenham, Hildegrade Schmidt, and Hector MacQueen. Each of the characters has their own peculiarities and their own reasons for being on the Orient Express. When Ratchett is murdered on board the train, it is Poirot's job to discern who the murderer is. In this case the conclusion is very unusual.<br /><br />On the whole, I enjoy reading Agatha Christie mysteries. This one in particular is iconic and makes for a good character study. It also helps to see the movie to better appreciate the subtleties of the book. The book is organized to a tee and draws the reader in to drawing their own conclusions before the end of the book. Astute readers will identify the culprit (probably early on in the book), but will still enjoy the book nonetheless. Agatha Christie maintains her reputation with each book she wrote. While the book is somewhat formulaic in the overall structure, it is nevertheless enjoyable.
